Its subcommittee may visit and inspect countries which have opted into the Optional Protocol.</li>
<li>The <i><a href="/wiki/Committee_on_the_Rights_of_the_Child" title="Committee on the Rights of the Child">Committee on the Rights of the Child</a></i> monitors the <a href="/wiki/Convention_on_the_Rights_of_the_Child" title="Convention on the Rights of the Child">CRC</a> and makes comments on reports submitted by states every five years. It does not have the power to receive complaints.</li>
<li>The <i>Committee on Migrant Workers</i> was established in 2004 and monitors the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Convention_on_the_Protection_of_the_Rights_of_All_Migrant_Workers_and_Members_of_Their_Families" class="mw-redirect" title="United Nations Convention on the Protection of the Rights of All Migrant Workers and Members of Their Families">ICRMW</a> and makes comments on reports submitted by states every five years. It will have the power to receive complaints of specific violations only once ten member states allow it.</li>
<li>The <i><a href="/wiki/Committee_on_the_Rights_of_Persons_with_Disabilities" title="Committee on the Rights of Persons with Disabilities">Committee on the Rights of Persons with Disabilities</a></i> was established in 2008 to monitor the <a href="/wiki/Convention_on_the_Rights_of_Persons_with_Disabilities" title="Convention on the Rights of Persons with Disabilities">Convention on the Rights of Persons with Disabilities</a>. It has the power to receive complaints against the countries which have opted into the <a href="/wiki/Optional_Protocol_to_the_Convention_on_the_Rights_of_Persons_with_Disabilities" title="Optional Protocol to the Convention on the Rights of Persons with Disabilities">Optional Protocol to the Convention on the Rights of Persons with Disabilities</a>.</li>
<li>The <i>Committee on Enforced Disappearances</i> monitors the <a href="/wiki/International_Convention_for_the_Protection_of_All_Persons_from_Enforced_Disappearance" title="International Convention for the Protection of All Persons from Enforced Disappearance">ICPPED</a>. All States parties are obliged to submit reports to the Committee on how the rights are being implemented. The Committee examines each report and addresses its concerns and recommendations to the State party in the form of "concluding observations”.</li>
</ul>
<p>Each treaty body receives secretariat support from the Human Rights Council and Treaties Division of Office of the High Commissioner on Human Rights (OHCHR) in Geneva except CEDAW, which is supported by the Division for the Advancement of Women (DAW). CEDAW formerly held all its sessions at United Nations headquarters in New York but now frequently meets at the United Nations Office in Geneva; the other treaty bodies meet in Geneva. The Human Rights Committee usually holds its March session in New York City.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Regional_human_rights_regimes">Regional human rights regimes</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Regional_human_rights_regimes" title="Regional human rights regimes">Regional human rights regimes</a></div>
<p>International human rights regimes are in several cases "nested" within more comprehensive and overlapping regional agreements. These regional regimes can be seen as relatively independently coherent human rights sub-regimes.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> Three principal regional human rights instruments can be identified; the <a href="/wiki/African_Charter_on_Human_and_Peoples%27_Rights" title="African Charter on Human and Peoples' Rights">African Charter on Human and Peoples' Rights</a>, the <a href="/wiki/American_Convention_on_Human_Rights" title="American Convention on Human Rights">American Convention on Human Rights</a> (the Americas) and the <a href="/wiki/European_Convention_on_Human_Rights" title="European Convention on Human Rights">European Convention on Human Rights</a>. The European Convention on Human Rights has since 1950 defined and guaranteed human rights and fundamental freedoms in Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-EUCourt_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EUCourt-68">[68]</a></sup> All 47 member states of the Council of Europe have signed the Convention and are therefore under the jurisdiction of the European Court of Human Rights in <a href="/wiki/Strasbourg" title="Strasbourg">Strasbourg</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-EUCourt_68-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EUCourt-68">[68]</a></sup></p>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_human_rights_articles_by_country" title="List of human rights articles by country">List of human rights articles by country</a> and <a href="/wiki/National_human_rights_institutions" class="mw-redirect" title="National human rights institutions">National human rights institutions</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Human_rights_promotion">Human rights promotion</span></h3>
<p>Human rights continue to be promoted around the world through governmental organizations and museums including the <a href="/wiki/Canadian_Museum_for_Human_Rights" title="Canadian Museum for Human Rights">Canadian Museum for Human Rights</a> in <a href="/wiki/Winnipeg" title="Winnipeg">Winnipeg</a>, <a href="/wiki/Manitoba" title="Manitoba">Manitoba</a>.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Non-governmental_actors">Non-governmental actors</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Human_rights_group" title="Human rights group">Human rights group</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Non-governmental_organizations">Non-governmental organizations</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Athens_Pride_2013,_Electra_Leda_Koutra_5.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Athens_Pride_2013%2C_Electra_Leda_Koutra_5.JPG/220px-Athens_Pride_2013%2C_Electra_Leda_Koutra_5.JPG" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Athens_Pride_2013%2C_Electra_Leda_Koutra_5.JPG/330px-Athens_Pride_2013%2C_Electra_Leda_Koutra_5.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Athens_Pride_2013%2C_Electra_Leda_Koutra_5.JPG/440px-Athens_Pride_2013%2C_Electra_Leda_Koutra_5.JPG 2x" data-file-width="4912" data-file-height="3264" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Athens_Pride_2013,_Electra_Leda_Koutra_5.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Visitors to an <a href="/wiki/LGBT_Pride" class="mw-redirect" title="LGBT Pride">LGBT Pride</a> event in Greece interact with members of a non-governmental human rights organization.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Non-governmental_organization" title="Non-governmental organization">Non-governmental organization</a></div>
<p>International <b>non-governmental human rights organizations</b> such as <a href="/wiki/Amnesty_International" title="Amnesty International">Amnesty International</a>, <a href="/wiki/Human_Rights_Watch" title="Human Rights Watch">Human Rights Watch</a>, <a href="/wiki/International_Service_for_Human_Rights" title="International Service for Human Rights">International Service for Human Rights</a> and <a href="/wiki/International_Federation_of_Human_Rights" class="mw-redirect" title="International Federation of Human Rights">FIDH</a> monitor what they see as human rights issues around the world and promote their views on the subject. Human rights organizations have been said to "translate complex international issues into activities to be undertaken by concerned citizens in their own community".<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> Human rights organizations frequently engage in <a href="/wiki/Lobbying" title="Lobbying">lobbying</a> and <a href="/wiki/Advocacy" title="Advocacy">advocacy</a> in an effort to convince the United Nations, supranational bodies and national governments to adopt their policies on human rights. Many human-rights organizations have observer status at the various UN bodies tasked with protecting human rights. A new (in 2009) non-governmental human-rights conference is the <a href="/wiki/Oslo_Freedom_Forum" title="Oslo Freedom Forum">Oslo Freedom Forum</a>, a gathering described by <a href="/wiki/The_Economist" title="The Economist">The Economist</a> as "on its way to becoming a human-rights equivalent of the Davos economic forum." The same article noted that human-rights advocates are more and more divided amongst themselves over how violations of human rights are to be defined, notably as regards the Middle East.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup></p>
<p>There is criticism of human-rights organizations who use their status but allegedly move away from their stated goals. For example, <a href="/wiki/Gerald_M._Steinberg" title="Gerald M. Steinberg">Gerald M. Steinberg</a>, an Israel-based academic, maintains that NGOs take advantage of a "<a href="/wiki/Halo_effect" title="Halo effect">halo effect</a>" and are "given the status of impartial moral watchdogs" by governments and the media.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> Such critics claim that this may be seen at various governmental levels, including when human-rights groups testify before investigation committees.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Human_rights_defenders">Human rights defenders</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Human_rights_defender" class="mw-redirect" title="Human rights defender">Human rights defender</a></div>
<p>A human rights defender is someone who, individually or with others, acts to promote or protect human rights. Human rights defenders are those men and women who act peacefully for the promotion and protection of those rights, and most of this activity happens within a nation as opposed to internationally.<sup id="cite_ref-twsGaryJBass_6-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twsGaryJBass-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Corporations">Corporations</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Multinational_corporation" title="Multinational corporation">Multinational companies</a> play an increasingly large role in the world, and have been responsible for numerous human rights abuses.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> Although the legal and moral environment surrounding the actions of governments is reasonably well developed, that surrounding multinational companies is both controversial and ill-defined.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2008)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Multinational companies' primary responsibility is to their <a href="/wiki/Shareholder" title="Shareholder">shareholders</a>, not to those affected by their actions. Such companies may be larger than the economies of some of the states within which they operate, and can wield significant economic and political power. No international treaties exist to specifically cover the behaviour of companies with regard to human rights, and national legislation is very variable. <a href="/wiki/Jean_Ziegler" title="Jean Ziegler">Jean Ziegler</a>, Special Rapporteur of the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Commission_on_Human_Rights" title="United Nations Commission on Human Rights">UN Commission on Human Rights</a> on the right to food stated in a report in 2003:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>[T]he growing power of transnational corporations and their extension of power through privatization, deregulation and the rolling back of the State also mean that it is now time to develop binding legal norms that hold corporations to human rights standards and circumscribe potential abuses of their position of power.</p>
<div class="templatequotecite"><cite>— Jean Ziegler<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup></cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p>In August 2003 the Human Rights Commission's Sub-Commission on the Promotion and Protection of Human Rights produced draft <i>Norms on the responsibilities of transnational corporations and other business enterprises with regard to human rights</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> These were considered by the Human Rights Commission in 2004, but have no binding status on corporations and are not monitored.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Violations">Violations</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/War" title="War">War</a>, <a href="/wiki/Genocides_in_history" title="Genocides in history">Genocides in history</a>, <a href="/wiki/Human_rights_defender" class="mw-redirect" title="Human rights defender">Human rights defender</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Corporate_accountability_for_human_rights_violations" title="Corporate accountability for human rights violations">Corporate accountability for human rights violations</a></div>
<div class="hatnote selfreference">See also: <a href="/wiki/Category:Human_rights_abuses" title="Category:Human rights abuses">Category:Human rights abuses</a>.</div>
<p><b>Human rights violations</b> occur when actions by state (or non-state) actors abuse, ignore, or deny basic human rights (including civil, political, cultural, social, and economic rights). Furthermore, violations of human rights can occur when any state or non-state actor breaches any part of the UDHR treaty or other international human rights or humanitarian law.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> In regard to human rights violations of <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a> laws, Article 39 of the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Charter" title="United Nations Charter">United Nations Charter</a> designates the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Security_Council" title="United Nations Security Council">UN Security Council</a> (or an appointed authority) as the only tribunal that may determine UN human rights violations.</p>
<p>Human rights abuses are monitored by United Nations committees, national institutions and governments and by many independent <a href="/wiki/Non-governmental_organization" title="Non-governmental organization">non-governmental organizations</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Amnesty_International" title="Amnesty International">Amnesty International</a>, <a href="/wiki/International_Federation_of_Human_Rights" class="mw-redirect" title="International Federation of Human Rights">International Federation of Human Rights</a>, <a href="/wiki/Human_Rights_Watch" title="Human Rights Watch">Human Rights Watch</a>, <a href="/wiki/World_Organisation_Against_Torture" title="World Organisation Against Torture">World Organisation Against Torture</a>, <a href="/wiki/Freedom_House" title="Freedom House">Freedom House</a>, <a href="/wiki/International_Freedom_of_Expression_Exchange" class="mw-redirect" title="International Freedom of Expression Exchange">International Freedom of Expression Exchange</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anti-Slavery_International" title="Anti-Slavery International">Anti-Slavery International</a>. These organizations collect evidence and documentation of alleged human rights abuses and apply pressure to enforce human rights laws.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Wars_of_aggression" class="mw-redirect" title="Wars of aggression">Wars of aggression</a>, <a href="/wiki/War_crime" title="War crime">war crimes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Crime_against_humanity" class="mw-redirect" title="Crime against humanity">crimes against humanity</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Genocide" title="Genocide">genocide</a>, are breaches of <a href="/wiki/International_humanitarian_law" title="International humanitarian law">International humanitarian law</a> and represent the most serious of human rights violations.</p>
<p>In efforts to eliminate violations of human rights, building awareness and protesting inhumane treatment has often led to calls for action and sometimes improved conditions. The UN Security Council has interceded with peace keeping forces, and other states and treaties (NATO) have intervened in situations to protect human rights.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Substantive_rights">Substantive rights</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Right_to_life">Right to life</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Right_to_life" title="Right to life">Right to life</a></div>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>Every human being has the inherent right to life. This right shall be protected by law. No one shall be arbitrarily deprived of his life.</p>
<div class="templatequotecite"><cite>— Article 6.1 of the International Covenant on Civil and Political Rights</cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p>The right to life is the essential right that a human being has the right not to be killed by another human being. The <a href="/wiki/Concept" title="Concept">concept</a> of a right to life is central to debates on the issues of <a href="/wiki/Abortion" title="Abortion">abortion</a>, <a href="/wiki/Capital_punishment" title="Capital punishment">capital punishment</a>, <a href="/wiki/Euthanasia" title="Euthanasia">euthanasia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Self-defense_(theory)" class="mw-redirect" title="Self-defense (theory)">self defence</a> and <a href="/wiki/Just_War" class="mw-redirect" title="Just War">war</a>. According to many human rights activists, the <a href="/wiki/Death_penalty" class="mw-redirect" title="Death penalty">death penalty</a> violates this right.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a> has called on states retaining the death penalty to establish a moratorium on capital punishment with a view to its abolition.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> States which do not do so face considerable moral and political pressure.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Freedom_from_torture">Freedom from torture</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Torture" title="Torture">Torture</a></div>
<p>Throughout history, torture has been used as a method of political <a href="/wiki/Mind_control" title="Mind control">re-education</a>, interrogation, punishment, and coercion. In addition to state-sponsored torture, individuals or groups may be motivated to inflict torture on others for similar reasons to those of a state; however, the motive for torture can also be for the <a href="/wiki/Sadomasochism" title="Sadomasochism">sadistic</a> gratification of the torturer, as in the <a href="/wiki/Moors_murders" title="Moors murders">Moors murders</a>.</p>
<p>Since the mid-20th century, torture is prohibited under <a href="/wiki/International_law" title="International law">international law</a> and the domestic laws of most countries. It is considered to be a violation of human rights, and is declared to be unacceptable by Article 5 of the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">UN</a> <a href="/wiki/Universal_Declaration_of_Human_Rights" title="Universal Declaration of Human Rights">Universal Declaration of Human Rights</a>. Signatories of the <a href="/wiki/Geneva_Conventions" title="Geneva Conventions">Geneva Conventions of 1949</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Protocol_I" title="Protocol I">Additional Protocols I</a> and <a href="/wiki/Protocol_II" title="Protocol II">II</a> of June 8, 1977 officially agree not to torture captured persons in <a href="/wiki/Armed_conflicts" class="mw-redirect" title="Armed conflicts">armed conflicts</a>, whether international or internal. Torture is also prohibited by the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Convention_Against_Torture" class="mw-redirect" title="United Nations Convention Against Torture">United Nations Convention Against Torture</a>, which has been ratified by 157 countries.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup></p>
<p>National and international legal prohibitions on torture derive from a consensus that torture and similar ill-treatment are immoral, as well as impractical.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> Despite these international conventions, organizations that monitor abuses of human rights (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Amnesty_International" title="Amnesty International">Amnesty International</a>, the <a href="/wiki/International_Rehabilitation_Council_for_Torture_Victims" title="International Rehabilitation Council for Torture Victims">International Rehabilitation Council for Torture Victims</a>) report widespread use condoned by states in many regions of the world.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> Amnesty International estimates that at least 81 world governments currently practise torture, some of them openly.<sup id="cite_ref-Amnesty08_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Amnesty08-83">[83]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Freedom_from_slavery">Freedom from slavery</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Slavery" title="Slavery">Slavery</a></div>
<p>Freedom from slavery is internationally recognized as a human right. Article 4 of the <a href="/wiki/Universal_Declaration_of_Human_Rights" title="Universal Declaration of Human Rights">Universal Declaration of Human Rights</a> states:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>No one shall be held in slavery or servitude; slavery and the slave trade shall be prohibited in all their forms.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Despite this, the number of slaves today is higher than at any point in <a href="/wiki/History_of_slavery" title="History of slavery">history</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-time_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-time-85">[85]</a></sup> remaining as high as 12 <a href="/wiki/Million" title="Million">million</a><sup id="cite_ref-Forced_labour_.E2.80.93_Themes_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Forced_labour_.E2.80.93_Themes-86">[86]</a></sup> to 27 million,<sup id="cite_ref-disposable-bales_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-disposable-bales-87">[87]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-un.org_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-un.org-88">[88]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BBC_Millions_.27forced_into_slavery.27_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC_Millions_.27forced_into_slavery.27-89">[89]</a></sup> Most are <a href="/wiki/Debt_bondage" title="Debt bondage">debt slaves</a>, largely in <a href="/wiki/South_Asia" title="South Asia">South Asia</a>, who are under <a href="/wiki/Debt_bondage" title="Debt bondage">debt bondage</a> incurred by <a href="/wiki/Loan_sharks" class="mw-redirect" title="Loan sharks">lenders</a>, sometimes even for generations.<sup id="cite_ref-newint_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-newint-90">[90]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Human_trafficking" title="Human trafficking">Human trafficking</a> is primarily for prostituting <a href="/wiki/Woman" title="Woman">women</a> and <a href="/wiki/Child" title="Child">children</a> into <a href="/wiki/Prostitution" title="Prostitution">sex industries</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-voa_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-voa-91">[91]</a></sup></p>
<p>Groups such as the <a href="/wiki/American_Anti-Slavery_Group" title="American Anti-Slavery Group">American Anti-Slavery Group</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anti-Slavery_International" title="Anti-Slavery International">Anti-Slavery International</a>, <a href="/wiki/Free_the_Slaves" title="Free the Slaves">Free the Slaves</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Anti-Slavery_Society" title="Anti-Slavery Society">Anti-Slavery Society</a>, and the Norwegian Anti-Slavery Society continue to campaign to rid the world of slavery.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Right_to_a_fair_trial">Right to a fair trial</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Right_to_a_fair_trial" title="Right to a fair trial">Right to a fair trial</a></div>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>Everyone is entitled in full equality to a fair and public hearing by an independent and impartial tribunal, in the determination of his rights and obligations and of any criminal charge against him.<sup id="cite_ref-Article_10_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Article_10-92">[92]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>The right to a fair trial has been defined in numerous regional and <a href="/wiki/International_human_rights_instruments" title="International human rights instruments">international human rights instruments</a>. It is one of the most extensive human rights and all international human rights instruments enshrine it in more than one article.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> The right to a fair trial is one of the most litigated human rights and substantial case law has been established on the interpretation of this human right.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> Despite variations in wording and placement of the various fair trial rights, international human rights instrument define the right to a fair trial in broadly the same terms.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> The aim of the right is to ensure the proper administration of justice. As a minimum the right to fair trial includes the following fair trial rights in <a href="/wiki/Civil_law_(common_law)" title="Civil law (common law)">civil</a> and <a href="/wiki/Criminal_law" title="Criminal law">criminal</a> proceedings:<sup id="cite_ref-Doebbler_2006_108_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Doebbler_2006_108-96">[96]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li>the right to be heard by a competent, independent and impartial tribunal</li>
<li>the right to a public hearing</li>
<li>the right to be heard within a reasonable time</li>
<li>the <a href="/wiki/Right_to_counsel" title="Right to counsel">right to counsel</a></li>
<li>the right to interpretation<sup id="cite_ref-Doebbler_2006_108_96-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Doebbler_2006_108-96">[96]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Freedom_of_speech">Freedom of speech</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_speech" title="Freedom of speech">Freedom of speech</a></div>
<p>Freedom of speech is the freedom to speak freely without censorship. The term freedom of expression is sometimes used synonymously, but includes any act of seeking, receiving and imparting information or ideas, regardless of the medium used. In practice, the right to freedom of speech is not absolute in any country and the right is commonly subject to limitations, such as on libel, slander, obscenity, incitement to commit a crime, etc. The right to freedom of expression is recognized as a human right under Article 19 of the Universal Declaration of Human Rights and recognized in international human rights law in the International Covenant on Civil and Political Rights (ICCPR). Article 19 of the ICCPR states that "[e]veryone shall have the right to hold opinions without interference" and "everyone shall have the right to freedom of expression; this right shall include freedom to seek, receive and impart information and ideas of all kinds, regardless of frontiers, either orally, in writing or in print, in the form of art, or through any other media of his choice".</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Freedom_of_thought.2C_conscience_and_religion">Freedom of thought, conscience and religion</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_thought" title="Freedom of thought">Freedom of thought</a>, <a href="/wiki/Conscience" title="Conscience">Conscience</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_religion" title="Freedom of religion">Freedom of religion</a></div>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>Everyone has the right to freedom of thought, conscience and religion; this right includes freedom to change his religion or belief, and freedom, either alone or in community with others and in public or private, to manifest his religion or belief in teaching, practice, worship and observance.</p>
<div class="templatequotecite"><cite>— Article 18 of the International Covenant on Civil and Political Rights</cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p>Freedom of thought, conscience and religion are closely related rights that protect the <a href="/wiki/Political_freedom" title="Political freedom">freedom</a> of an individual or community, in public or private, to think and freely hold conscientious beliefs and to manifest <a href="/wiki/Religion" title="Religion">religion</a> or <a href="/wiki/Belief" title="Belief">belief</a> in <a href="/wiki/Religious_education" title="Religious education">teaching</a>, practice, <a href="/wiki/Worship" title="Worship">worship</a>, and observance; the concept is generally recognized also to include the freedom to <a href="/wiki/Religious_conversion" title="Religious conversion">change religion</a> or <a href="/wiki/Irreligion_by_country" class="mw-redirect" title="Irreligion by country">not to follow any religion</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> The freedom to <i>leave</i> or discontinue membership in a religion or religious group—in religious terms called "<a href="/wiki/Apostasy" title="Apostasy">apostasy</a>"—is also a fundamental part of religious freedom, covered by Article 18 of the <a href="/wiki/Universal_Declaration_of_Human_Rights" title="Universal Declaration of Human Rights">Universal Declaration of Human Rights</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup></p>
<p>Human rights groups such as <a href="/wiki/Amnesty_International" title="Amnesty International">Amnesty International</a> organizes campaigns to protect those arrested and or incarcerated as a <a href="/wiki/Prisoner_of_conscience" title="Prisoner of conscience">prisoner of conscience</a> because of their conscientious beliefs, particularly concerning intellectual, political and artistic freedom of expression and association.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> In legislation, a <a href="/wiki/Conscience_clause_(medical)" title="Conscience clause (medical)">conscience clause</a> is a provision in a statute that excuses a health professional from complying with the law (for example legalizing surgical or pharmaceutical <a href="/wiki/Abortion" title="Abortion">abortion</a>) if it is incompatible with religious or conscientious beliefs.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Freedom_of_movement">Freedom of movement</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_movement" title="Freedom of movement">Freedom of movement</a></div>
<p>Freedom of movement asserts that a citizen of a state in which that citizen is present has the liberty to travel, reside in, and/or work in any part of the state where one pleases within the limits of respect for the liberty and rights of others,<sup id="cite_ref-Gilbert_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gilbert-101">[101]</a></sup> and to leave that state and return at any time.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rights_debates">Rights debates</span></h3>
<p>Events and new possibilities can affect existing rights or require new ones. Advances of technology, medicine, and philosophy constantly challenge the <a href="/wiki/Status_quo" title="Status quo">status quo</a> of human rights thinking.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Right_to_keep_and_bear_arms">Right to keep and bear arms</span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Right_to_keep_and_bear_arms" title="Right to keep and bear arms">Right to keep and bear arms</a></div>
<p>The right to keep and bear arms for defence is described in the philosophical and political writings of Aristotle, Cicero, John Locke, Machiavelli, the <a href="/wiki/Whig_(British_political_faction)" class="mw-redirect" title="Whig (British political faction)">English Whigs</a> and others.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> In countries with an English <a href="/wiki/Common_law" title="Common law">common law</a> tradition, a long-standing common law right to keep and bear arms has long been recognized, as pre-existing in common law, prior even to the existence of national constitutions.<sup id="cite_ref-nclr_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nclr-103">[103]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Future_generations">Future generations</span></h4>
<p>In 1997, <a href="/wiki/UNESCO" title="UNESCO">UNESCO</a> adopted the <i>Declaration on the Responsibilities of the Present Generation Towards the Future Generation</i>. The Declaration opens with the words:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>Mindful of the will of the peoples, set out solemnly in the <a href="/wiki/Charter_of_the_United_Nations" class="mw-redirect" title="Charter of the United Nations">Charter of the United Nations</a>, to 'save succeeding generations from the scourge of war' and to safeguard the values and principles enshrined in the <a href="/wiki/Universal_Declaration_of_Human_Rights" title="Universal Declaration of Human Rights">Universal Declaration of Human Rights</a>, and all other relevant instruments of international law.</p>
<div class="templatequotecite"><cite>— Declaration on the Responsibilities of the Present Generation Towards the Future Generation</cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p>Article 1 of the declaration states "the present generations have the responsibility of ensuring that the needs and interests of present and future generations are fully safeguarded." The preamble to the declaration states that "at this point in history, the very existence of humankind and its environment are threatened" and the declaration covers a variety of issues including protection of the <a href="/wiki/Natural_environment" title="Natural environment">environment</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Human_genome" title="Human genome">human genome</a>, <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity" title="Biodiversity">biodiversity</a>, cultural heritage, <a href="/wiki/Peace" title="Peace">peace</a>, development, and <a href="/wiki/Education" title="Education">education</a>. The preamble recalls that the responsibilities of the present generations towards future generations has been referred to in various international instruments, including the Convention for the Protection of the World Cultural and Natural Heritage (UNESCO 1972), the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Framework_Convention_on_Climate_Change" title="United Nations Framework Convention on Climate Change">United Nations Framework Convention on Climate Change</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Convention_on_Biological_Diversity" title="Convention on Biological Diversity">Convention on Biological Diversity</a> (Rio de Janeiro, 1992), the <a href="/wiki/Rio_Declaration_on_Environment_and_Development" title="Rio Declaration on Environment and Development">Rio Declaration on Environment and Development</a> (UN Conference on Environment and Development, 1992), the <a href="/wiki/Vienna_Declaration_and_Programme_of_Action" title="Vienna Declaration and Programme of Action">Vienna Declaration and Programme of Action</a> (World Conference on Human Rights, 1993) and a number of UN General Assembly resolutions relating to the protection of the global climate for present and future generations adopted since 1990.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Sexual_orientation_and_gender_identity">Sexual orientation and gender identity</span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/LGBT_rights_by_country_or_territory" title="LGBT rights by country or territory">LGBT rights by country or territory</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:232px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Anjali_gopalan.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Anjali_gopalan.jpg/230px-Anjali_gopalan.jpg" width="230" height="205" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Anjali_gopalan.jpg/345px-Anjali_gopalan.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Anjali_gopalan.jpg/460px-Anjali_gopalan.jpg 2x" data-file-width="714" data-file-height="635" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Anjali_gopalan.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Asia's first <a href="/wiki/Genderqueer" title="Genderqueer">Genderqueer</a> Pride Parade at <a href="/wiki/Madurai" title="Madurai">Madurai</a> with <a href="/wiki/Anjali_Gopalan" title="Anjali Gopalan">Anjali Gopalan</a>. On December 11, 2013, homosexuality was criminalized in <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a> by a Supreme Court ruling.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Sexual orientation and gender identity rights relate to the expression of <a href="/wiki/Sexual_orientation" title="Sexual orientation">sexual orientation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gender_identity" title="Gender identity">gender identity</a> based on the right to respect for private life and the right not to be discriminated against on the ground of "other status" as defined in various human rights conventions, such as article 17 and 26 in the United Nations <a href="/wiki/International_Covenant_on_Civil_and_Political_Rights" title="International Covenant on Civil and Political Rights">International Covenant on Civil and Political Rights</a> and <a href="/wiki/Article_8_of_the_European_Convention_on_Human_Rights" title="Article 8 of the European Convention on Human Rights">article 8</a> and <a href="/wiki/European_Convention_on_Human_Rights#Article_14_-_discrimination" title="European Convention on Human Rights">article 14</a> in the European Convention on Human Rights.</p>
<p>As of 2011, homosexual behaviour is illegal in 76 countries and punishable by execution in seven countries.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> The criminalization of private, consensual, adult sexual relations, especially in countries where corporal or capital punishment is involved, is one of the primary concerns of LGBT human rights advocates.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup></p>
<p>Other issues include: government recognition of <a href="/wiki/Civil_union_in_the_United_States" class="mw-redirect" title="Civil union in the United States">same-sex relationships</a>, <a href="/wiki/LGBT_adoption" title="LGBT adoption">LGBT adoption</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sexual_orientation_and_military_service" title="Sexual orientation and military service">sexual orientation and military service</a>, immigration equality, <a href="/wiki/Anti-discrimination_laws" class="mw-redirect" title="Anti-discrimination laws">anti-discrimination laws</a>, hate crime laws regarding violence against LGBT people, <a href="/wiki/Sodomy_laws" class="mw-redirect" title="Sodomy laws">sodomy laws</a>, anti-lesbianism laws, and equal age of consent for same-sex activity.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup></p>
<p>A global charter for sexual orientation and gender identity rights has been proposed in the form of the '<a href="/wiki/Yogyakarta_Principles" title="Yogyakarta Principles">Yogyakarta Principles</a>', a set of 29 principles whose authors say they apply <a href="/wiki/International_Human_Rights_Law" class="mw-redirect" title="International Human Rights Law">International Human Rights Law</a> statutes and precedent to situations relevant to LGBT people's experience.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> The principles were presented at a <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a> event in New York on November 7, 2007, co-sponsored by <a href="/wiki/Argentina" title="Argentina">Argentina</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a> and <a href="/wiki/Uruguay" title="Uruguay">Uruguay</a>.</p>
<p>The principles have been acknowledged with influencing the French proposed <a href="/wiki/Sexual_orientation_and_gender_identity_at_the_United_Nations" class="mw-redirect" title="Sexual orientation and gender identity at the United Nations">UN declaration on sexual orientation and gender identity</a>, which focuses on ending violence, criminalization and capital punishment and does not include dialogue about same-sex marriage or right to start a family.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup> The proposal was supported by 67 of the then 192 member countries of the United Nations, including all <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">EU</a> member states and the United States. An alternative statement opposing the proposal was initiated by <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a> and signed by 57 member nations, including all 27 nations of the <a href="/wiki/Arab_League" title="Arab League">Arab League</a> as well as <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a> and <a href="/wiki/North_Korea" title="North Korea">North Korea</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Trade">Trade</span></h4>
<p>Although both the <a href="/wiki/Universal_Declaration_of_Human_Rights" title="Universal Declaration of Human Rights">Universal Declaration of Human Rights</a> and the <a href="/wiki/International_Covenant_on_Economic,_Social_and_Cultural_Rights" title="International Covenant on Economic, Social and Cultural Rights">International Covenant on Economic, Social and Cultural Rights</a> emphasize the importance of a right to work, neither of these documents explicitly mention free trade as a mechanism for ensuring this fundamental right. And yet trade plays a key role in providing jobs.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some experts argue that trade is inherent to human nature and that when governments inhibit international trade they directly inhibit the right to work and the other indirect benefits, like the right to education, that increased work and investment help accrue.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> Others have argued that the ability to trade does not affect everyone equally—often groups like the rural poor, indigenous groups and women are less likely to access the benefits of increased trade.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup></p>
<p>On the other hand, others think that it is no longer primarily individuals but companies that trade, and therefore it cannot be guaranteed as a human right.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2008)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Additionally, trying to fit too many concepts under the umbrella of what qualifies as a human right has the potential to dilute their importance. Finally, it is difficult to define a right to trade as either "fair"<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup> or "just" in that the current trade regime produces winners and losers but its reform is likely to produce (different) winners and losers.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup></p>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/The_Recognition_of_Labour_Standards_within_the_World_Trade_Organization" class="mw-redirect" title="The Recognition of Labour Standards within the World Trade Organization">The Recognition of Labour Standards within the World Trade Organization</a> and <a href="/wiki/Investor_state_dispute_settlement" class="mw-redirect" title="Investor state dispute settlement">Investor state dispute settlement</a></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Water">Water</span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Right_to_water" title="Right to water">Right to water</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Water_politics" title="Water politics">Water politics</a></div>
<p>The right to water has been recognized in a wide range of international documents, including treaties, declarations and other standards. For instance, the 1979 Convention on the Elimination of All Forms of Discrimination Against Women (<a href="/wiki/CEDAW" class="mw-redirect" title="CEDAW">CEDAW</a>) requires State parties to ensure to women the right to “enjoy adequate living conditions, particularly in relation to […] water supply”. The 1989 Convention on the Rights of the Child (CRC) requires States parties to combat disease and malnutrition “through the provision of adequate nutritious foods and clean drinking-water”.</p>
<p>The most clear definition of the Human right to water has been issued by the UN Committee on Economic, Social and Cultural Rights. This treaty body interpreting legal obligations of State parties to the International Covenant on Economic, Social and Cultural Rights (<a href="/wiki/ICESCR" class="mw-redirect" title="ICESCR">ICESCR</a>) issued in 2002 a non-binding interpretation affirming that access to water was a condition for the enjoyment of the right to an adequate standard of living and inextricably related to the right to the highest attainable standard of health (see ICESCR Art.11 &amp; 12) and therefore a human right:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>The human right to water entitles everyone to sufficient, safe, acceptable, physically accessible and affordable water for personal and domestic uses.</p>
<div class="templatequotecite"><cite>— United Nations Committee on Economic, Social and Cultural Rights</cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p>On July 28, 2010, the United Nations General Assembly declared water and sanitation as human rights. Today all States have at least ratified one human rights convention which explicitly or implicitly recognizes the right, and they all have signed at least one political declaration recognizing this right.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Sexual_and_reproductive_rights">Sexual and reproductive rights</span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Reproductive_rights" title="Reproductive rights">reproductive rights</a></div>
<p>Human rights include women’s rights and sexual and reproductive rights. Sexual and reproductive rights are part of a continuum of human rights, which includes the rights to life, health and education, the rights to equality and non-discrimination, and the right to decide the timing, number and spacing of one’s children.<sup id="cite_ref-unfpa.org_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-unfpa.org-124">[124]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-COOK_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-COOK-125">[125]</a></sup></p>
<p>Reproductive and sexual rights as part of human rights was affirmed internationally at the Programme of Action of the International Conference on Population and Development (ICPD) in Cairo in 1994.<sup id="cite_ref-unfpa.org_124-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-unfpa.org-124">[124]</a></sup> It was the first among international development frameworks to address issues related to sexuality, sexual and reproductive health, and reproductive rights.<sup id="cite_ref-Commitment_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Commitment-126">[126]</a></sup></p>
<p>The ICPD Programme of Action in paragraph 7.2 “defines an individual’s sexual and reproductive health as complete well-being related to sexual activity and reproduction. Sexual and reproductive health and rights (SRHR) encompass both entitlements and freedoms. This includes the definition of reproductive rights in paragraph 7.3 of the ICPD PoA, which clarifies that these are not a new set of rights but human rights in existing human rights instruments related to sexual and reproductive autonomy and the attainment of sexual and reproductive health.<sup id="cite_ref-Reproductive_rights_127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reproductive_rights-127">[127]</a></sup> Additionally, the 1995 Beijing Platform for Action (PfA) expands this definition to cover both sexuality and reproduction by affirming in paragraph 96 the right to exercise control over and make decisions about one’s sexuality, including sexual and reproductive health, free of coercion, discrimination and violence.” <sup id="cite_ref-Commitment_126-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Commitment-126">[126]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="World_Health_Organization">World Health Organization</span></h4>
<p>Reproductive rights were first established as a subset of human rights at the United Nations 1968 International Conference on Human Rights.<sup id="cite_ref-FREEDMAN_128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FREEDMAN-128">[128]</a></sup> The sixteenth article of the resulting Proclamation of Teheran states, "Parents have a basic human right to determine <a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_will" class="mw-redirect" title="Freedom of will">freely</a> and <a href="/wiki/Moral_responsibility" title="Moral responsibility">responsibly</a> the number and the spacing of their children."<sup id="cite_ref-FREEDMAN_128-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FREEDMAN-128">[128]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-teheran_proc_129-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-teheran_proc-129">[129]</a></sup></p>
<p>Reproductive rights may include some or all of the following rights: the right to legal or safe <a href="/wiki/Abortion" title="Abortion">abortion</a>, the right to <a href="/wiki/Birth_control" title="Birth control">control one's reproductive functions</a>, the right to quality <a href="/wiki/Reproductive_health" title="Reproductive health">reproductive healthcare</a>, and the right to <a href="/wiki/Family_planning" title="Family planning">education and access</a> in order to make reproductive choices free from <a href="/wiki/Coercion" title="Coercion">coercion</a>, <a href="/wiki/Discrimination" title="Discrimination">discrimination</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Violence" title="Violence">violence</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-AMNESTY_130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AMNESTY-130">[130]</a></sup></p>
<p>Reproductive rights may also be understood to include <a href="/wiki/Sex_education" title="Sex education">education</a> about <a href="/wiki/Contraception" class="mw-redirect" title="Contraception">contraception</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sexually_transmitted_disease" class="mw-redirect" title="Sexually transmitted disease">sexually transmitted infections</a>, and freedom from <a href="/wiki/Compulsory_sterilization" title="Compulsory sterilization">coerced sterilization</a> and contraception, protection from gender-based practices such as <a href="/wiki/Female_genital_cutting" class="mw-redirect" title="Female genital cutting">female genital cutting</a> (FGC) and <a href="/wiki/Male_genital_mutilation" class="mw-redirect" title="Male genital mutilation">male genital mutilation</a> (MGM).<sup id="cite_ref-COOK_125-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-COOK-125">[125]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FREEDMAN_128-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FREEDMAN-128">[128]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-AMNESTY_130-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AMNESTY-130">[130]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Information_and_communication_technologies">Information and communication technologies</span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Right_to_Internet_access" title="Right to Internet access">Right to Internet access</a> and <a href="/wiki/Digital_rights" title="Digital rights">Digital rights</a></div>
<p>In October 2009, <a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">Finland</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Transport_and_Communications_(Finland)" title="Ministry of Transport and Communications (Finland)">Ministry of Transport and Communications</a> announced that every person in Finland would have the legal right to Internet access.<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup> Since July 2010, the government has legally obligated <a href="/wiki/Telecommunications_companies" class="mw-redirect" title="Telecommunications companies">telecommunications companies</a> to offer broadband Internet access to every permanent residence and office. The connection must be "reasonably priced" and have a downstream rate of at least 1 Mbit/s.<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup></p>
<p>In March 2010, the <a href="/wiki/BBC" title="BBC">BBC</a>, having commissioned an <a href="/wiki/Opinion_poll" title="Opinion poll">opinion poll</a>, reported that "almost four in five people around the world believe that access to the internet is a fundamental right."<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup> The poll, conducted by the polling company <a href="/wiki/GlobeScan" title="GlobeScan">GlobeScan</a> for the <a href="/wiki/BBC_World_Service" title="BBC World Service">BBC World Service</a>, collated the answers of 27,973 adult citizens across 26 countries to find that 79% of adults either strongly agreed or somewhat agreed with the statement: "access to the internet should be a fundamental right of all people".<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Right_to_Non-refoulement_vs._Right_to_Asylum">Right to Non-refoulement vs. Right to Asylum</span></h4>
<p>Non-refoulement is the right not to be returned to a place of persecution and is the foundation for international refugee law, as outlined in the 1951 Convention Relating to the Status of Refugees.<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup> Both the right to non-refoulement and the right to asylum have taken centre stage in recent debates over the treatment of refugees. A central worry about the right to asylum is that it can limit a states power to handle a mass influx of refugees.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup> Processing asylum applications can take a considerable amount of time, and this amount rises with the amount of refugees applying. This creates an incentive for more refugees to apply, since they are allowed to stay in the country during the application process. One potential solution to the problem of mass influx is proposed by political philosopher Andy Lamey. Lamey proposes a portable procedural model that focuses on the right to non-refoulement.<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup> Crucially, the procedural rights defended by this model can be applied outside national borders, within any rights respecting country; this allows the burden of mass influx to be shared by a plurality of countries without violating the procedural rights of the refugee.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Relationship_with_other_topics">Relationship with other topics</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_environment">The environment</span></h3>
<p>There are two basic conceptions of environmental human rights in the current human rights system. The first is that the right to a healthy or adequate environment is itself a human right (as seen in both Article 24 of the <a href="/wiki/African_Charter_on_Human_and_Peoples%27_Rights" title="African Charter on Human and Peoples' Rights">African Charter on Human and Peoples' Rights</a>, and Article 11 of the San Salvador Protocol to the <a href="/wiki/American_Convention_on_Human_Rights" title="American Convention on Human Rights">American Convention on Human Rights</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup> The second conception is the idea that environmental human rights can be derived from other human rights, usually – the right to life, the right to health, the right to private family life and the right to property (among many others). This second theory enjoys much more widespread use in human rights courts around the world, as those rights are contained in many human rights documents.</p>
<p>The onset of various <a href="/wiki/List_of_environmental_issues" title="List of environmental issues">environmental issues</a>, especially <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">climate change</a>, has created potential conflicts between different human rights. Human rights ultimately require a working ecosystem and healthy environment, but the granting of certain rights to individuals may damage these. Such as the conflict between right to decide number of offspring and the common need for a healthy environment, as noted in the <a href="/wiki/Tragedy_of_the_commons" title="Tragedy of the commons">tragedy of the commons</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-hardin68_141-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hardin68-141">[141]</a></sup> In the area of environmental rights, the responsibilities of multinational corporations, so far relatively unaddressed by human rights legislation, is of paramount consideration.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2008)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Environmental_rights" class="mw-redirect" title="Environmental rights">Environmental rights</a> revolve largely around the idea of a right to a livable environment both for the present and the future generations.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="National_security">National security</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/National_security" title="National security">National security</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anti-terrorism_legislation" title="Anti-terrorism legislation">Anti-terrorism legislation</a></div>
<p>With the exception of non-derogable human rights (international conventions class the right to life, the right to be free from slavery, the right to be free from torture and the right to be free from retroactive application of penal laws as non-derogable),<sup id="cite_ref-resourceII_142-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-resourceII-142">[142]</a></sup> the UN recognizes that human rights can be limited or even pushed aside during times of national emergency – although</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>the emergency must be actual, affect the whole population and the threat must be to the very existence of the nation. The declaration of emergency must also be a last resort and a temporary measure.</p>
<div class="templatequotecite"><cite>— United Nations. <i>The Resource</i><sup id="cite_ref-resourceII_142-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-resourceII-142">[142]</a></sup></cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p>Rights that cannot be derogated for reasons of national security in any circumstances are known as <a href="/wiki/Peremptory_norm" title="Peremptory norm">peremptory norms or <i>jus cogens</i></a>. Such <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Charter" title="United Nations Charter">United Nations Charter</a> obligations are binding on all states and cannot be modified by treaty.</p>
<p>Examples of national security being used to justify human rights violations include the <a href="/wiki/Japanese_American_internment" class="mw-redirect" title="Japanese American internment">Japanese American internment</a> during <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup> Stalin's <a href="/wiki/Great_Purge" title="Great Purge">Great Purge</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup> and the modern-day abuses of terror suspects rights by some countries, often in the name of the <a href="/wiki/War_on_Terror" title="War on Terror">War on Terror</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Relativism_and_universalism">Relativism and universalism</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Cultural_relativism" title="Cultural relativism">Cultural relativism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Moral_relativism" title="Moral relativism">Moral relativism</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Moral_universalism" title="Moral universalism">Moral universalism</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:White_mans_burden_the_journal_detroit.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/White_mans_burden_the_journal_detroit.JPG/200px-White_mans_burden_the_journal_detroit.JPG" width="200" height="131" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/White_mans_burden_the_journal_detroit.JPG/300px-White_mans_burden_the_journal_detroit.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/White_mans_burden_the_journal_detroit.JPG/400px-White_mans_burden_the_journal_detroit.JPG 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="336" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:White_mans_burden_the_journal_detroit.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Relativists argue that human rights must avoid pushing the values of a single culture at the expense of others. "<a href="/wiki/The_White_Man%27s_Burden" title="The White Man's Burden">The White Man's Burden</a>" is seen as an example of the West using the spread of Western culture as a justification for colonization.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Fgm_map.gif" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Fgm_map.gif/200px-Fgm_map.gif" width="200" height="191" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Fgm_map.gif/300px-Fgm_map.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/43/Fgm_map.gif 2x" data-file-width="320" data-file-height="306" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Fgm_map.gif" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Universalists argue that some practices violate the norms of all human cultures. They point out that although <a href="/wiki/Female_genital_mutilation" title="Female genital mutilation">Female genital mutilation</a> is prevalent in Africa, no religion supports the practice, and the tradition is in violation of women's rights.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The UDHR enshrines universal rights that apply to all humans equally, whichever geographical location, state, race or culture they belong to. However, in academia there is a dispute between scholars that advocate <a href="/wiki/Moral_relativism" title="Moral relativism">moral relativism</a> and scholars that advocate <a href="/wiki/Moral_universalism" title="Moral universalism">moral universalism</a>. Relativists do not argue against human rights, but concede that human rights are <a href="/wiki/Social_constructionism" title="Social constructionism">socially constructed</a> and are shaped by cultural and environmental contexts. Universalists argue that human rights have always existed, and apply to all people regardless of culture, race, sex, or religion.</p>
<p>More specifically, proponents of cultural relativism argue for acceptance of different cultures, which may have practices conflicting with human rights. Relativists caution that universalism could be used as a form of cultural, economic or political imperialism. <a href="/wiki/The_White_Man%27s_Burden" title="The White Man's Burden">The White Man's Burden</a> is used as an example of imperialism and the destruction of local cultures justified by the desire to spread <a href="/wiki/Eurocentrism" title="Eurocentrism">Eurocentric</a> values.<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup> In particular, the concept of human rights is often claimed to be fundamentally rooted in a politically <a href="/wiki/Liberalism" title="Liberalism">liberal</a> outlook which, although generally accepted in Europe, <a href="/wiki/Human_rights_in_Japan" title="Human rights in Japan">Japan</a> or North America, is not necessarily taken as standard elsewhere.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Opponents of relativism argue that some practices exist that violate the norms of all human cultures. A common example is <a href="/wiki/Female_genital_mutilation" title="Female genital mutilation">female genital mutilation</a>, which occurs in different cultures in Africa, Asia and South America<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>. It is not mandated by any religion, but has become a tradition in many cultures. It is considered a violation of women's and girl's rights by much of the international community, and is outlawed in some countries.</p>
<p>The former Prime Ministers of <a href="/wiki/Singapore" title="Singapore">Singapore</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lee_Kuan_Yew" title="Lee Kuan Yew">Lee Kuan Yew</a>, and of <a href="/wiki/Malaysia" title="Malaysia">Malaysia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mahathir_bin_Mohamad" class="mw-redirect" title="Mahathir bin Mohamad">Mahathir bin Mohamad</a> both claimed in the 1990s that <i><a href="/wiki/Asian_values" title="Asian values">Asian values</a></i> were significantly different from Western values and included a sense of loyalty and foregoing personal freedoms for the sake of social stability and prosperity, and therefore authoritarian government is more appropriate in Asia than democracy. <a href="/wiki/Lee_Kuan_Yew" title="Lee Kuan Yew">Lee Kuan Yew</a> argued that:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>What Asians value may not necessarily be what Americans or Europeans value. Westerners value the freedoms and liberties of the individual. As an Asian of Chinese cultural background, my values are for a government which is honest, effective, and efficient.</p>
<div class="templatequotecite"><cite>— <a href="/wiki/Lee_Kuan_Yew" title="Lee Kuan Yew">Lee Kuan Yew</a>, 'Democracy, Human Rights and the Realities', Tokyo, Nov 10, 1992<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup></cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p>In response, critics have pointed out that cultural relativism could be used as a justification for authoritarianism. An example is in 1981, when the Iranian representative to the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a>, Said Rajaie-Khorassani, articulated the position of his country regarding the Universal Declaration of Human Rights by saying that the UDHR was "a <a href="/wiki/Secularism" title="Secularism">secular</a> understanding of the <a href="/wiki/Judeo-Christian" title="Judeo-Christian">Judeo-Christian</a> tradition", which could not be implemented by Muslims without trespassing the Islamic law.<sup id="cite_ref-Littman1999_149-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Littman1999-149">[149]</a></sup> The Asian Values argument was criticized by Mahathir's former deputy:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>To say that freedom is Western or unAsian is to offend our traditions as well as our forefathers, who gave their lives in the struggle against tyranny and injustices.</p>
<div class="templatequotecite"><cite>— <a href="/wiki/Anwar_Ibrahim" title="Anwar Ibrahim">A. Ibrahim</a> in his keynote speech to the Asian Press Forum title <i>Media and Society in Asia</i>, December 2, 1994</cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p>and by Singapore's opposition leader <a href="/wiki/Chee_Soon_Juan" title="Chee Soon Juan">Chee Soon Juan</a>, who states that it is racist to assert that Asians do not want human rights.<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup></p>
<p>Defenders of moral universalism argue that relativistic arguments neglect the fact that modern human rights are new to all cultures, dating back no further than the UDHR in 1948. They argue that the UDHR was drafted by people from many different cultures and traditions, including a US Roman Catholic, a Chinese Confucian philosopher, a French zionist and a representative from the Arab League, amongst others, and drew upon advice from thinkers such as Mahatma Gandhi.<sup id="cite_ref-Gready34_46-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gready34-46">[46]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Michael_Ignatieff" title="Michael Ignatieff">Michael Ignatieff</a> has argued that cultural relativism is almost exclusively an argument used by those who wield power in cultures which commit human rights abuses, and that those whose human rights are compromised are the powerless.<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup> This reflects the fact that the difficulty in judging universalism versus relativism lies in who is claiming to represent a particular culture.</p>
<p>Although the argument between universalism and relativism is far from complete, it is an academic discussion in that all international human rights instruments adhere to the principle that human rights are universally applicable. The <a href="/wiki/2005_World_Summit" title="2005 World Summit">2005 World Summit</a> reaffirmed the international community's adherence to this principle:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>The universal nature of human rights and freedoms is beyond question.</p>
<div class="templatequotecite"><cite>— 2005 World Summit, paragraph 121</cite></div>
</blockquote>

<li id="cite_note-AMNESTY-130"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-AMNESTY_130-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-AMNESTY_130-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.amnestyusa.org/Stop_Violence_Against_Women_SVAW/Reproductive_Rights/page.do?id=1108242&amp;n1=3&amp;n2=39&amp;n3=1101">"Stop Violence Against Women: Reproductive rights"</a>. Amnesty International USA. 2007<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">December 8,</span> 2007</span>. <q>Reproductive rights – access to sexual and reproductive healthcare and autonomy in sexual and reproductive decision-making – are human rights; they are universal, indivisible, and undeniable. These rights are founded upon principles of human dignity and equality, and have been enshrined in international human rights documents.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AHuman+rights&amp;rft.btitle=Stop+Violence+Against+Women%3A+Reproductive+rights&amp;rft.date=2007&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.amnestyusa.org%2FStop_Violence_Against_Women_SVAW%2FReproductive_Rights%2Fpage.do%3Fid%3D1108242%26n1%3D3%26n2%3D39%26n3%3D1101&amp;rft.pub=Amnesty+International+USA&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span></span></li>
